0452289963|1.0|The writing style lacks a structured flow, which makes his book really difficult to read, so much so, that a number of people of my acquaintance, have started the book, and then decided not to finish it at all. This lack of logical flow has the author saying the same things over and over, for page after page, and reaching conclusions, seemingly out of nowhere, because he does not explain his sources, or the thinking processes which lead him to those conclusions - most of these conclusions are simply presented as pronouncements from the `Authority'.<br /><br />The author writes in esoteric-sounding terms, using extremely vague language, so as to create the impression that he is operating at some exalted level from which he can talk down to his readers, many of whom, according to the author, are simply too unintelligent to understand his message--although, true to form, he insults these readers in vague terms by stating that those who do not understand him are not ready for enlightenment. Saying in effect, \\"Any lack of understanding is due to your inability to rise to my level, not my inability to stoop to yours.\\" After all, he wants these unintelligent people to buy his book so that they have the chance to read it, profess to understand it, and thus prove that they are not too unintelligent after all. Being completely unable to explain himself (perhaps because he is not yet ready to understand himself? Or perhaps because he has switched off his thinking mind, as he instructs his disciples to do?), he waffles on and on page after page, chapter after chapter saying nothing of any substance whatsoever, leaving the reader's head spinning, thinking, \\"What! What is he saying?\\" For example, he speaks at great length about `enlightenment', referring to it as `enlightenment', `awakening' and `salvation', but offers no definition of this term so that his readers might recognise this state if they stumbled upon it. This is especially important because the word `enlightenment' is a word in everyday use, and could have infinite shades of meaning. Instead the subject is covered in the vaguest terms possible, so that a thousand different readers might have a thousand different interpretations of what the author's version of enlightenment is. One only has to read the book reviews to see how confused, intolerant and dogmatic his disciples are--yet, just like their master, completely unable to explain his philosophy in anything remotely resembling practical terms. One of these disciples told me that the message is extremely simple and can be conveyed in one single sentence. Well, then why on earth does the author need more than four hundred pages to write this one sentence? And why does the author need to write book after book after book to convey this simple, one sentence message?<br />Tolle tells his disciples that the ego is the great enemy, yet he simply cannot prevent his own ego from trying to inflate his image even more by giving his story one more tweak here and one more tweak there. The problem with this little ego trip is that it gets him into a few holes from which he cannot escape. One such hole is first claiming to have been a regular person just like the rest of us before his spontaneous enlightenment--so regular in fact, that he sank into deep depression. But he simply could not resist claiming to have read Stephen Hawking's mind while he (Tolle) was still just a regular, unenlightened person. This little(!) slip of the ego generates some very interesting possibilities:<br />* He was never like us--he has always had special abilities,<br />* Operating at this exalted level, brought on, or at the very least, could not prevent his severe depression,<br />* If he was always at this higher level than the rest of us, then either his story of spontaneous enlightenment is a lie, or this spontaneous enlightenment raised him to an even higher level,<br />* If he could read such a brilliant mind as Stephen Hawking, he might be implying thathis own mind was even more brilliant,<br />* If his mind was more brilliant than Stephen Hawking even before he became enlightened, one can only imagine how far ahead of Stephen Hawking he is now. Small wonder he cannot understand his own thoughts well enough to explain them,<br />* He is simply lying about being able to read Stephen Hawking's mind.<br />\\"Oh what a tangled web we weave, when first we practice to deceive\\" (Sir Walter Scott).<br />Another example of his out of control ego getting him into a deep hole, is when he claims to be smarter than God--but, I'll get to that shortly.<br /><br />Tolle seems to be trying to suggest that enlightenment is an aloof, indifference from a perspective above one's thoughts, denying them and spending one's life in this state of denial [the only way I have ever achieved this (I want to say `experienced this' but in fact, one does not experience this mindless state - one realises that one has experienced it only after the event - but during the event, that part of one that records (consciously) the experience, is switched off, so one does not experience it, nor can one describe it) this thoughtless state, is doing meditation, where the conscious mind switches off. Sometimes, after repeated (in the same day) sessions of meditation, there is a lethargic, mental indifference, which lasts for hours, during which time one's rational mind is not fully on, and one's rational thinking is impaired - this is not an unpleasant state, but is not conducive to performing one's daily duties effectively. First of all, this state is not accessible by merely realising, \\"I am not this body, etc.\\" - this does not switch off your thinking mind - it takes much, much more than that, which is why the ancients invented meditation, and why adepts meditate for most of their day for many years - if simply knowing they were not their thoughts (which they know first-hand) were enough to cause enlightenment, then why would they meditate away years of their lives? Secondly, because we have daily duties and obligations, walking around in a half-minded stupor with our rational minds switched off, is counter-productive and dangerous.<br /><br />Similar ideas have surfaced throughout history, but the situations in which these have been applied have been very different to a modern, Western lifestyle. Practitioners who did not have families to support, mortgages to pay, and who could retreat from the world of daily stress, into monasteries and isolated mountain caves where they would meditate for twelve to eighteen hours a day for years on end to stop their thoughts. Even without all our possessions, responsibilities and lifestyle, these people could not simply stop their thoughts as Tolle suggests - so to imagine that we can do so, is a complete denial of reality. Furthermore, to state that simply realising that we are not our thoughts, puts us on the path to enlightenment, is just plain nonsense, because it does nothing to alter the constant relentless onslaught of millions upon millions of thoughts throughout any given day.<br /><br />The author talks about the importance of living in the now, and not in the past or future, and that the future never comes, because when it does, it is simply now again. Again, this is meant to make him sounds so clever, but it is in fact impossible, because every decision one makes now, has an impact on a future now, and when that impact occurs, it occurs because of one's analysis of information from a past now - time never stops. So, long before one has finished analysing new information, that information has become part of the past, and by analysing that past information, in order to make a decision, one is living in both the past and the future, because the outcome of one's decision now, will of necessity manifest itself in the future. Simply put, we are in time, and we cannot escape from it, and playing silly, pedantic games with words serves only to confound people but has no practical value at all.<br /><br />Not satisfied to portray himself as a higher being than us mortals, Tolle portrays himself as the new messiah, but not just the new messiah, the new, improved messiah, because he says that he is not making the same mistake that Jesus made--or, if you believe that God sent Jesus, then Tolle is saying that he is not making the same mistake that God made: Tolle claims to be appointed to deliver the exact same message as Jesus, but states that whereas Jesus delivered his message at the wrong time, he is delivering the message at exactly the right time. The ramifications of this claim are simply astounding:<br />We have two basic premises to consider:<br />1. Jesus was sent by God<br />2. Jesus was not sent by God<br />If Jesus was sent by God, either:<br />1. God made a mistake, sending His son at the wrong time<br />2. God sent His son at the wrong time on purpose--to prepare the way for Tolle 2,000 years later<br />If Jesus was not sent by God, then:<br />1. Of what value is this message?<br />2. Tolle claims to bring the exact, same message, therefore his message is also not from God.<br />One can almost imagine Tolle telling God, \\"Listen dad, don't screw up again like you did with Jesus--don't waste my time by sending me before my disciples are ready.\\"<br />Tolle cannot have it both ways--he cannot claim to bring a message from almighty God, then claim that this almighty God makes mistakes which automatically disqualifies him from being almighty God, and hence, automatically disqualifies Tolle's message from being from almighty God.<br />\\"Oh what a tangled web we weave, when first we practice to deceive\\" (Sir Walter Scott).<br /><br />Or, one can save a huge amount of time, cut through all the nonsense, and ask the obvious, logical question: If we were meant to ignore out thoughts, why do we have rational, thinking minds, designed to do nothing other than think--did our Creator make a (another) mistake?<br />Or why did we evolve rational, thinking minds if we did not need them?<br /><br />Surely, the quickest and surest way of achieving the mindless state waffled about by the author, would be to have a lobotomy--that would put an end to that damned thinking once and for all.

1610390938|5.0|Banerjee and Duflo write beautifully. This book is an easy read that summarizes an astounding number of studies of the microeconomics of poverty in developing countries. Despite not having one single sweeping conclusion, the authors manage to synthesize the literature into a compelling and insightful narrative of the lives of the very poor. The authors use almost no economic jargon and yet are able to accurately convey important and sophisticated economic concepts. It is quite a feat to be able to write in a way that can be understood and enjoyed by people with no background in economics as well as PhD economists.<br /><br />I had heard about randomized controlled trials (RCTs) in development economics. This book improved my view of them substantially; while I had a prior as to their usefulness, my prior was not very well informed. While it is true that the trials are not replicable and require external validity, Banerjee and Duflo are acutely aware of this limitation. Furthermore, many of the experiments are reproduced in other settings suggesting that much of the time the results do have external validity. When a seemingly similar experiment yields different results, the hope is that there is something to be learned from that. Banerjee and Duflo discuss cases of both types.<br /><br />I do worry that the very high cost of running such experiments may narrow the set of researchers that are able to publish development studies in the top academic journals. As such, we may be narrowing the range of viewpoints (e.g., macroeconomic perspectives) on development. I hope development economists working on RCTs continue to be open to other approaches of looking at development.<br /><br />Perhaps the most useful insight I took away from the book is the point that a lot of the same behavioral biases that cause the poor to make suboptimal decisions are shared by people in rich countries. How many times have you heard someone overweight, or who smokes, or who doesn't save enoughfor their retirement complain that the poor still have flatscreen TVs? The consequences of biases in decision-making and the processing of information are just much more severe for the poor. Rich people simply don't have many life or death choices (e.g., whether to purify drinking water) in their daily lives. This is a crucial point and should enable us to think about poverty with a little more compassion.<br /><br />I recommend this book to anyone who has even a passing interest in poverty and development.

006202406X|4.0|I was shocked by the heated reviews that came so soon after the book was released. Seems most of the negative reviews are readers who are angry by how Roth handled the relationship of the main characters. What has been lost is plot commentary and there is plenty to talk about. Some spoilers ahead...<br /><br />This is the final book in the Divergent Trilogy. A story about a dystopian Chicago that has been turned into a genetic experiment, one of several in that part of the United States. In the final book our heroes find themselves outside the walls of the city and face to face with the scientists who have been watching their every more. They learn of the country's history of genetic wars and are told that their city has been the most successful of all experimental cities to date. What they also find, however, is that the behavior of those outside the city is as bad, if not worse then those within. Citizens have been divided into genetically pure (divergent) and genetically damaged (everyone else). Tris and friends have escaped one revolution and have stumbled into yet another as the damaged, like the factionless, are preparing to rise up.<br /><br />Unlike the two previous books, Allegiant is told in alternating voices. We hear from both Tris and Tobias. Roth does not handle the change of voice well, and as many reviewers have noted, the voices sound alike and it is often difficult to remember which character is speaking. I found it very difficult to read Tobias' view as I had already created a voice for him in my head. I found Four to be weak which did not match his character from the two previous books.<br /><br />There is an influx of new characters and a dropping of those from the previous books. I believe that Roth made a huge mistake when she left rich characters such as Marcus and Evelyn behind in favor of new one dimensional villains. There were so many rich characters in the two previous books and we simply lost them. Even those who left the city with Trisand Tobias (Peter, Christine, Caleb) were left behind as we saw so little of their personalities.<br /><br />The story concept was very strong but Roth tried to handle too much in one book and lost the magic that made the Divergent series so wonderful. I did give it 4 starts because I thought that the story concept was sound, just handled badly, and the book is worth reading for those new concepts introduced. The divergent books could have been extended beyond three and this would have allowed Roth to incorporate the new ideas solidly into the story line.<br /><br />I would have preferred 3.5 stars so I rounded up.

006202406X|1.0|While I know that everyone is fuming about the ending, to me, the ending is the strongest part of the book... And that's really saying something.<br /><br />Let me start off by saying that the Divergent series (until now) was my Harry Potter. There was nothing I cared about more than these books. I've re-read Divergent and Insurgent more times than I can count. And at first, when I started reading Allegiant, I thought that maybe that was why I didn't like it. Maybe I had been waiting too long, maybe I had too high of expectations... But after getting half-way through the book, I realized that that wasn't it at all. The book is just plain bad. Like, really, really not good. The characters' voices sound absolutely nothing like they did in the previous books. They are completely and utterly unrecognizable. And, while I was first thrilled at the idea of dual chapters between Tris and Tobias, I quickly realized that there was absolutely no difference in their voices. I'd be reading a Tobias chapter and think it was Tris, and so on. It got to the point where I thought that this book must be written by someone else. That was the only explanation for why it was so different and bad compared to the other two books (which are flawless in my eyes). But alas, it was actually written by Roth, which leaves a hole in my gut. I couldn't even finish the book, it was that bad. I didn't care about the characters anymore because they weren't the characters I cared about before.<br /><br />But besides the characters, the story was full of more plot holes than plot lines. It was repetitive, monotone, boring, and lifeless. There was way too much going on for it's own good and it asked more questions than it answered. So after desperately trying to like this book and failing miserably, I quit and skipped to the end, just to find out how she concluded a series that was once so dear to my heart. And once I realized what happened, I wasn't even mad. I think Tris' actions at the end of the book are the only thing in the entire novel that stay true to character.<br /><br />So basically, I made this review to say that we need to stop focusing on the ending as the only bad point of this book. Truth be told, the book was a wreck from the first chapter and a shocking ending doesn't change that. It's not a matter of being upset and sad over the end, it's a matter of good versus bad writing. And while the first two books were phenomenal, this book was anything but that.<br /><br />It's with a heavy heart that I say R.I.P to the Divergent series that I once loved more than anything else. Veronica Roth, I don't know what happened when you were writing this book, all I know is that I wish you'd never wrote it at all.

006202406X|4.0|** SPOILER-FREE REVIEW **<br /><br />It took me a long time to realize this series is not about a dystopian society or war or the dangers of scientific progress. Rather, one of the biggest themes in the series is the power of choice. The characters, especially Tris, learn how to choose love, forgiveness and sacrifice instead of the easy way out. What unnerved me most (in a good way) was that Tris was always a completely normal girl, but she continually chose to act extraordinarily. As the reader, I felt inadequate next to her, knowing that she had no super powers which I could use to distance her choices from my own. The other characters make some pretty heavy decisions too, and it is nice to see how much they have matured from the previous books.<br /><br />I was less pleased with the corrupt government/revolution storyline in this series, the justification for the faction society setup, and the scientific reasons that led to and perpetuated animosity between divergent and non-divergent. I understand the parallels that Roth meant to draw between her world and ours, but I could never really suspend my disbelief for it (probably because I have taken a few genetics courses that delved into the nature/nurture argument).<br /><br />Overall, I enjoyed the lessons within this novel, and look forward to reading more of Veronica Roth's work.

B00BD99JMW|2.0|***SPOILER ALERT****<br /><br />I am giving this 2 stars as I reserve the lowest star for books without flow or character development.  I don't often write reviews as I have a very high respect for art and dislike chewing a piece apart simply because I have an opinion about it.  (This is also not the best review--I am not a writer or a teacher so take from it what you will.) However, this novel was such a disappointment for me.  I agree wholeheartedly with the numerous reviews that have also scored this book low.<br /><br />There is no distinct character voice between Tris & Four.  If you disagree, read partway into a chapter and put it down for a couple of days or overnight.  When you pick it back up and read a few paragraphs, a few pages even, can you tell who is narrating?  I couldn't!<br /><br />There are numerous character development flaws/inconsistencies.  These are mentioned in previous reviews and I don't have the heart to retype them here.  One major inconsistency and the biggest problem I have with the climax event is that to the core, more than she was selfless, Tris was a fighter.  And Roth had her choose to fight up until the very end where she so easily gives up because she see's her Mother?  I just didn't buy it.  I might have been able to justify Roth's motives to kill Tris off, if she hadn't taken her fight from her at the end.  And why did she kill her off?  What did it accomplish except to rob hope from and create anguish for the reader?  If I wanted to experience those feelings, I would choose to read a Jodi Picoult novel.<br /><br />I desperately tried to justify the ending of this book and have tried to reason it several times.  One of the reasons I read fiction is to discover new perspective's that can help enrich my world.  I believe that one of the essential elements of good fiction is a sense of realistic character development that allows me to identify with the main character.  I think Roth did a decent job of that early on in the seriesbut not with this novel.  What new take away message is the author giving us?  How is she improving/enriching the reader's world by creating this void? If you've lived for a certain amount of time you learn to understand the meaning of the phrase: life is unfair.  We are constantly thrown challenges that shape us; we are robbed and robbed again of so many things throughout life and it is a never ending process.  At the end of the day, each of us holds on to some type of hope--even in grief.<br /><br />I think Roth tried to put the novel back together by giving Four the grieving process.  But replicating the grieving process is an extremely sensitive and time consuming event and Roth didn't give it the extreme attention it needed to help the reader resolve his/her own loss of Tris.  Roth spent an entire 2 novels creating a link between Tris and the reader.  Killing her off so quickly severed the link as sharply as it severed the link between Tris and Four.  At the end of it all, and still days later, I have no sense of closure from the void that this novel created.  It would have been better if I had not read it.

B00005JK8K|3.0|John Woo was a hot director in Hong Kong and when he had to flee, Hollywood was happy to snatch him up. The transition was rough and he didn't translate well. \\"Windtalkers\\" is evidence of that.<br /><br />Here's the scoop. During WWII the U.S. military began using Navajo Indians to speak code so the enemy not only can't break it because of the code. They can't break the language. Nicholas Cage is a sergeant assigned to one of the Navajo Windtalkers to protect him and make certain he doesn't fall into enemy hands and divulge the code. Cage has some serious baggage so the task proves a difficult one.<br /><br />This is a very uneven film. Woo, known for his directing of action scenes, does a poor job in the opening scene. It's stiff and clunky. But then the action shifts to Sai Pan and he really steps up and delivers some of the best battle scenes ever filmed. But then there's the script. I'm guessing Woo, not having a good enough ear for the English language, didn't know when the script contained some really clunky, cliche ridden dialogue and situations. Between the battle scenes there are attempts to flesh out the story and characters that have been done way too times. Also, given the set-up, the viewer knows there are only a couple of ways this can end. It's merely a matter of which way the writers want the coin to fall. The acting is decent enough. Nick Cage is about as good as you're going to see him.The rest of the roles are played decent enough, but no one looks as though they're fishing for an academy award.<br /><br />This is an interesting chapter in history. They just needed a better script and some more even handed directing to bring it to life.

B00EGMQIJ0|5.0|This is a well-written and engaging novel that moves along to what appears as an increasingly inevitable conclusion.  The basic theme is that information and surveillance technology will soon be all-encompassing and, if we allow it, there will be no such thing as privacy.  Much of the book consists of the gullible protagonist accepting specious arguments for complete transparency.  The idea is that if there is right to provide a public record of, for example, sex offenders, why not drunken drivers, why not people with parking tickets? Crucially, the bad guys sell all this as a set of \\"rights\\".  If you have the flu, don't your neighbors have a right to know? Although written as satire too much of what the author satirizes is too close to the truth.  Pervasive surveillance is becoming a reality not only for drone strikes in Pakistan but for many public spaces in Europe and the US (almost all in London).  If you still think Eggers is fear-mongering take a look at AutoTrack (the personal database, not the vehicle tracking version).  If you can, get a look at law-enforcement version.  If that doesn't scare you its hard to imagine what would.
